RATING: 8
This is quickly becoming one of my favorite games.  The play feels fast with 2, because there is always more you want to do, and there is just the right amount of confrontation for me.

Something about the game reminds me of Tigris and Euphrates, except less aggressive and thematically more integrated and immersive. I can't wrap my head around T&E, but La Citta's play is intuitive and absorbing.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A civilization tile-laying game.

The initial setup of the game is similar to Settlers of Catan in that you want to make your initial placements near the best resources.  Then the gameplay involves playing actions (3 of which are "free") through card play.  Your goal is to build up large, strong cities, that you can still maintain well enough to feed your people, and keep them happy.  To do this, you need to have enough food, the right type of buildings, and of course you need enough money to continue to maintain everything.

There are many opportunities to both gain and lose people from competitors cities, and having people is where most of the points come from, and a loss at the right time and/or part of your city can be devastating.  

This is a quality design, and it is very strategic, but it has two significant components to the game (purchased actions, and the voice of the people) that are random card-draws, which can be a bit unnerving for such a long strategy game.  What I found interesting is that I was losing the game for the first 5 rounds, and ended up winning in the 6th and final round.  Even with that, it felt like a game I respected rather than enjoyed playing, but this may have been due to the extremely long length of the game.

RATING: 8
A good game that isn't quite what it appears to be. It seems to be all about city building... and it is, but not like other city building games. 

There are no resources to produce other than food and money. It's ultimately all about the *influence* of your cities. The goal is to attract and KEEP the biggest population--because how you build your cities will eventually set you up to either steal or lose citizens to neighboring cities. It mostly boils down to a war of tactically oriented influence and supply chains. 

Beyond that, I like the way actions work and the 'voice of the people' creates a neat dynamic.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Quite good! Very fun. I added a bunch of black, blue, yellow, and white 30 sided dice so that metrics can be tracked for each city on the field (counting and recounting everything was a negative strike against it in the reviews I read), which adds a couple pounds to the box.

It nicely balances quality of options with simplicity of teaching the rules. Given that both I and my brother liked it, I think that it would go over well with almost anyone.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I find La Citta oddly reminiscent of Age of Steam.  There is the same tension between the growth necessary for victory and outstripping the resources to support it. The central mechanic in La Citta is emigration from your neighbour's dusty town to your gleaming city state.  Unfortunately, new citizens are also new mouths to feed and the penalties for letting people starve are suitably severe. Also like Age of Steam, La Citta is absolutely unforgiving; an early mistake can lose you the game.  Oh and there's no gradual build up in the contest, both games are a bare knuckle fight from the very start.

It is true that La Citta has a significant number of random or incalculable elements.  Chief among the latter is the inability to predict how many citizens your city will gain or lose this year with any precision.  The political cards also seem poorly balanced; the master builder is almost always taken as soon as he appears, rich harvests tend to just clutter up the display. 

Artwork and production quality are both very good.  The available English translation of the rules is serviceable but not an easy read.

RATING: 6
Frustrating and fiddly.  The "Voice of the people" mechanic is a good idea, used in the wrong game in the wrong way.  It makes the game annoyingly random: each "year" takes 20 minutes of gameplay, then whether it was a success or a failure comes down to the flip of a card.  The bizarre rule that action cards no-one wants clog up the table for the entire rest of the game means a poor choice of useful action cards, increasing randomness.
Disappointing since there are some good elements here (for example, the layout of the board is ingeniously designed and perfectly suited to its intention.)

RATING: 5
Rating based on just one play.  

I have to admit that I enjoyed playing this, but I think there are a lot of problems.  It kept me engaged as I tried to figure out what to do with my five actions, balancing food, gold production, city expansion, and competition with neighboring cities.  There were usually many things that needed to be done, and I needed to prioritize based on what I thought my neighbors would do.

But those elements can be found in better games.

1. I found myself constantly counting up my people to make sure I had enough food -- and not only the people I had, but my speculated population changes as well.  It's very necessary but tedious.

2. Seven special cards are always present, and you have to spend at least two actions a turn on them.  The problem is that they come up randomly, and bad cards don't get cleared out.  So we were often stuck with poor options unless the person before you goes and a new card comes up.

3. The central mechanic which dictates how people move from city to city is pretty random as well.  You can try to count cards or use an action to peek at what's coming, but mostly it's just random.

In the end I realized a few efficiency mistakes I made, but other than that the game didn't leave me with much to think about.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Recently revisited with 3P.  Past plays with 2P have yielded runaway leaders, and I'm still inclined to think that the game lends itself to that.  Hard to imagine playing with more than 3P because of game length, which didn't seem worth it for the game experience with that count.  Found the rules revisit relatively simple and umambiguous, if repetitive.  Love the bits.  Like the small mix of hidden, accessible information.  The action cards can pile up, especially at the game end (many seem to house rule it).  Found it a large advantage to be able to bleed out abandoned cities turn after turn, with the issue being that there's little to no incentive for an owner to combat it once it's started.  Although turns can be planned, the potential downtime between them really leads me to think this would be better as a PBW game.  Maybe SpielByWeb will really finish it, one day.

RATING: 9
My favourite city-building game where you need to control resources (especially food) and make sure that your citizens are well-supplied with art, hospitals and schools or else they well travel to a neighbouring city.

Long term planning needs to  be approached carefully or your expansion will suddenly hit a brick wall.  There are also countless opportunities to screw your opponents plans.

Only drawback is the countless hexagons representing improvements are double-sided demanding too much game time for sorting and searching.

RATING: 7.8
Medium euro, at the heart if which is a mechanism for building your cities and stealing people (=points) from cities nearby. The requirement to feed all the people you have (or face a harsh punisment) and build your cities functionally contends nicely against the requirement to outdo your fellow cities in the 3 resources (or the one which will be popular this turn) to ensure you steal people from them and not the other way round. A solid game.

RATING: 6
La Città is your typically serious, unforgiving, thinking-heavy eurogame where mirth is forbidden, laughs are uncommon and your only aim is to limit the damage you take.

Anyhow, it has two features I like: it employs nice miniatures instead of the usual wooden cubes and, unlike most such games, it even allows and fosters direct confrontation among players, although by bloodless and sly means only, which turns into a double-edged weapon, since you can both attract citizens from an opponent’s town this turn and then lose them to him the next turn.

Although it’s quite long, often clueless and definitely boring, all in all I found La Città slightly enjoyable.

RATING: 9
[bought German version punched but not played from a local guy I traded with]

I really like this game, just like I thought I would. The five actions per turn are just enough to make me feel like I'm accomplishing something, but still few enough to make me feel like I need more. The decisions are tough, and it's cool to see stuff spreading all over the board. I agreed that the UI of the game isn't great--it's hard to tell what's what and to count up all your people and resources and distinguish between the buildings and castles, etc., but I used hholzgrafe's excellent player aids, and it's certainly not enough to discourage me from playing this game again. 

I wasn't sure I'd want a "civ lite" game after I fell in love with and bought Through the Ages, but it's so completely different from that game that I needn't have worried. Big thumbs up on this one.

Have only played it 2-player, and I can see that there might be a runaway leader problem, but maybe not. It was a learning game for both of us. I'll need to play it again before I can tell if it's indicative of the game or if it was just a fluke. I think that up to four players it would be fine, but that the board would be too tight for my liking with five players. Maybe not. I wouldn't be averse to trying it.

UPDATE:
Have played it with 3 and 4 players now. It's great with any number so far. The board scales well. The only thing they don't scale is the number of building tiles, which can probably run out with 5 players and would add a whole new factor to the game. I've played three times in a week or so, and I still want to play it again soon. Great game!

The turns go pretty fast, because you have only so many choices, so there's not a lot of downtime.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
An empire building game - sort of. Forget the theme, it is basically irrelevant to the game. You are tasked with developing the two cities you start with so as to maximize their value and to have the largest population by the game end. Depending on how many players you have, the possibility of establishing up to two more cities may be feasible.

Limited action game regulated by a card draft and augmented by three cards each player has to use each turn. Players may draw population from opponent's cities depending on the type of infrastructure you have and the results of another separate card draw.

A fair amount of luck is generated by the card distribution and while there is ample room for strategy and tactics, one is always having to adjust to the changing situation created by the card flow. If you can live with that, the game works quite well and provides an interesting challenge. Four players can finish a game in a couple of hours.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is something of an economic engine game, with limited actions, and a direct competition mechanism whereby you are able to steal other player's citizens - this is very significant, since you must have a citizen for each tile in each of your cities, so stealing citizens reduces their ability to grow, or even causes some destruction of the city.  

The heart of the game is the Political round, where you choose 5 actions in five rounds.  You have 3 default actions, and the others come from 7 cards laid out on a display.  The actions available dictate what you can do.  Most of them allow you to do more if you pay for it, so gold becomes useful for accessing better actions.  

There is a food mechanic as well where you must produce as much food as you have citizens at the end of the round, with the penalty for failing being harsh enough that it is not a viable strategy to fail.  

Overall, the main goal of the game is to steal everyone else's citizens.  Citizens are the main source of victory points at the end.  You also get some from cities, but you'll get those if you've been doing what it takes to steal citizens.  The catch is that cities require a lot of "support" buildings in order to have just a few buildings that help you steal citizens.  So you spend most of your actions building infrastructure.  The rules are also structured such that it is quite likely that once you start having citizens stolen from one city, you might as well give up on it, and focus elsewhere.  It is not always true that you will not be able to defend, but it is true often enough to be frustrating.  And even if a successful defense is possible, it may not be a wise use of your limited resources.  

My first play was a 3 player, and, despite limited competition, it got a very poor response from the other 2 players.  I was solidly neutral on it.  I think that with enthusiastic veterans, there is probably a very tight game of maneuvering and backstabbing in here.  With anyone else, this is probably not worth playing.  It's just too tight, too unforgiving, and too opaque a game to be good on the first play, and I just don't play enough to be willing to invest several 2 hour sessions not having fun so that I can eventually enjoy it.

Overall, I will keep it for now and try to play one more time, but I don't see it remaining in my collection long term.

Update: Traded away
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
La citta es un juego de desarrollo de ciudades. El desarrollo se lleva a cabo mediante la construcción de diferentes tipos de edificios que aumentan el atractivo de la ciudad en tres aspectos: Salubridad, conocimiento y estética. En cada turno, está representada la voz del pueblo que determina cual de esos aspectos va a ser más valorado por los habitantes de las ciudades... y según consigas ciudades más adecuadas, provocaras movimientos migratorios de las ciudades vecinas hacia las tuyas. Pero ojo, las ciudades tienen que estar preparadas parael aumento de población y a toda esa gente hay que darle de comer. El objetivo es conseguir mayor población y ciudades con los tres atractivos. Perder gente por falta de comida en el ultimo turno te puede penalizar. 
Es un juego no demasiado complicado, pero bastante cerebral. El tablero se queda pequeño rapidamente y las decisiones hay que pensarlas bien. 

Todo un descubrimiento. Recomendable sin lugar a dudas.
Adquirido a Bicho por 30€ en agosto de 2011

RATING: 7.5
Very interesting, almost abstract city-builder that pushes you to grow but punishes you severely if you overextend yourself. Early in the game, you'll be desperate for water and money so you can expand your population/city with fountains/buildings, but later on, food will be your primary concern. Failure to feed your population can be devastating, and sometimes unintentional if you happen to win a couple of city challenges and suddenly find yourself short of food during scoring. 

As a result, the whole game becomes a delicate balancing act between wanting to shoot ahead in one area, only to discover it will punish you in another. I love the card market, which definitely changes your strategy when a good card comes up. Overall, it's a really neat design, and feels surprisingly fresh for such an old game.

RATING: 9
One of my favorite moderate-to-heavies.  Game has depth, but the rules flow in such a way that the game plays intuitively.  Gamers pick this up quickly, and I would hazard a guess that the rules are simple enough to maybe use la Citta' as a gateway game. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A nice, clever game of city-building (sort of). Although the play of the game is strictly I go-you go, it moves along pretty briskly for the most part as there are limited choices each turn and a fair number of moves will be "forced" (you have to build farms, quarries, etc). This is OK, as what you do with the left-overs is important and there is enough flexibility in timing of the stuff you have to do with respect to the stuff you want to do (you're going to have to build a farm, but you can put it off if something else good is available, or do it now and hope something better comes up).

Still, avoid with the AP types, there is significant potential for lock-up here. Also, it bears mentioning that the luck in the game is not trivial. The Voice of the People can be harsh. But one of the cool things about La Citta is that it *is* a harsh game, and keeping your people fed and happy is not easy, and if you blow it your cities can hemorrhage people and die.  This is kind of a nice break from the usual forgiving German games, and it's not over-done - the game is neither long nor harsh enough to have people kicked to the curb for too long.

Note on the 2007 reprint: I'm not totally enamored of the new language-independent cards; the old ones were clearer.

Unless you really become La Citta sharks, stick with the "beginner" setup. Unbalanced setups can lead to an uninteresting game, even more so than Settlers, and what makes a good setup is tricky. I've played this a fair amount and still always found the best games use the standard setups.

RATING: 7
Played it once to date with four players. Hope to get a second playing very soon. 

As opposed to Attika and a number of other games, the city building theme actually is both strong and well done.

The are a number of interesting building strategies and I find attaining the golden middle of controlled growth as opposed to demographic decline or overpopulation most interesting.

So, there is a lot to like. However, a bit of accounting and the danger of analysis paralysis may be offputting for some. The latter phenomenon really depends with whom you play. With slow players, there'll be too much downtime and the game longer than it ought to. We had a bit of that, but then most of us were unfamiliar with the game, so I hope next time we'll play, we'll do so at a brisker pace.

As for the raised criticism of runaway victories, well, I dare say in such a case the rest of the board simply aren't doing their job. We had a photo-finish and I believe with competent players games should be close and tense until the end.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
The game has an inherent run-away leader problem that is a natural consequence of the game's basic design. I discussed this issue on my blog in an article I wrote on the complications that result when a game implements resource attrition as a function of direct player interaction. I specifically used La Citta as an example. Check out the link here:

http://mike-compton.blogspot.com/2007/12/triangulation-issues-in-resource.html


I used to rate this game a "1" because I claimed it is broken. From one viewpoint it is. However, I also understand that other games with the same problem of resource attrition (i.e. Twilight Imperium) and multiple players in the game are not necessarily games that I rate a "1". This is due to the fact that a game's "brokenness" depends on your point of view. In games like Twilight Imperium, it becomes more about negotiation and less about equal opportunities for victory. So, I've upgraded my rating of La Citta to a 2.

RATING: 7
Punishing of mistakes, and long for a German game, but still fun. In the tradition of many great games there are many things you want to do, and no time to do them all. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
The more I play La Città, the more I'm beginning to feel that my initial enthusiasm wasn't warranted. At first I felt intrigued by the sprawling cities and opportunities to find sufficient food and water for the masses, but at the same time it's nearly all you do in this game. Plus, of course, getting money and erecting prestigeous buildings. For a game which lasts as long as it does, that's not much to keep the players occupied. What makes things worse is that the game gets in the way of the players at times. Take for example the Voice of the People: it takes a relatively large amount of actions to yield sufficient information; and if you know what the people think there is still the problem of acting on it without causing food shortages in your own cities. The political display tends to clog up with useless cards unless you play with a variant which sees a healthy rotation of them. And then the little meeples! The game looks very cool because of them, absolutely true, but the neverending associated counting and bookkeeping becomes a chore. Finally, the game seems to be prone to a sort of reversed runaway leader issue which reminds me of the process called Ostwald ripening: large cities are always more powerful than small ones, meaning that small cities stay small and tend to decrease in size while large cities stay large and tend to grow (albeit slowly) because of the attractive pull they exert. The action 'Found a New Castello' is therefore practically worthless after a while. In summary: although there are some nice aspects, there is plenty of roughly hewn stone which could stand a lot of finishing work.

Truth be told, I've always played the game using the standard setup, where I was able to more or less prove rigorously that players should always go for exactly 3 castellos. With 2 their empire grows too slowly, and with 4 it inevitably runs into food problems which offset the larger scale population growth. What I do not know is whether the full game, with randomly placed resource tiles, suffers from a similar problem or not. I suspect it does as the maths don't change, and in grain-rich areas there will be such strong competition that this is a limit in itself. If this is indeed the case, then La Città's rating will take a big nosedive.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 4
Components: Lots of sturdy components. The citizens stand up surprisingly well. The art is perhaps too detailed on the buildings, which are difficult to distinguish from each other.

Experience: Interesting game of civil growth. Having the three areas of emphasis voiced by the people is a simple, engaging way to let cities have an assortment of buildings, without each city having numerous special powers. I also appreciate that the voice of the people mechanism, along with the random assortment of cards up for grabs, helps to make each of my turns slightly different. However, overall I don't find the game to be too fun. The central mechanisms—budgeting for population and food, and trying to steal citizens from nearby cities—doesn't do much for me.

First Played: 2004-06-02
Last Played: 2005-02-20
Eagerness to Play Again: No Desire

3 players: 1

RATING: N/A
$30
Set in the Italian countryside, this game features various city-states vying for population. Each player is given two cities to start with, and is charged with expanding the different aspects of the cities to attract larger populations. Players build farms to feed their people, quarries to finance their expansions, marketplaces to allow growth in their cities, and bathhouses to keep their people clean and healthy. Additionally, structures can be built that give the city influence in one of three categories. Superiority in one of these categories will cause population to shift from other nearby cities when the Voice of the People is decided at the end of each turn. At the end of six turns, the player with the most victory points, which are determined from well-rounded cities, well-fed populations, and overall size of population, will be the winner.

I'm still exploring La Citta, but I'm liking it. There's strategy and interaction, math and second-guessing your opponents. I have only played two-player, and it would seem that the board does a great job adapting for the number of players. At first, this game might seem as if you have plenty of time to build up, but the reality is that the competition between cities comes quickly. That means you really have to watch, calculate and counteract your opponents' moves. If you don't, the game can be over pretty early because its hard to make a comeback.

RATING: 8
After a few plays I have decided to rate this game 8. It really falls in the category of games I like.

Pro´s:
+ There seem to be a lot of ways to win. For example in one game I had a very, very bad start with the city I wanted to use as a corn producer. One guy built into my influence region in the first round and started stealing my citizens. While I first thought that my game would be over after just a few minutes of play, I was able to adept my strategy and won in the end. So I can´t see the runaway-leader-problem some gamers are complaining about.

+ It is possible to play this game aggressively. Something I miss in so many other games.

+ The theme of the game is great and doesn´t feel pasted on. While playing, I really feel like I´m managing some cities.

+ The amount of luck is at the right level. Just like in games as Euphrates & Tigris and Wallenstein there are some things you don´t know for sure and you have to deal with this dilemma. The one who does this best normally wins.

Cons:
- This is not especially a problem I have with La Citta, it is more a general complaint which also applies to La Citta. The end of the game seems to come  at a random point. You play n rounds and then the game is over. I prefer it if the game comes to an end "naturally" like it happens in games like Chess or Hive.

Most of those cities are going to be worth 3 points.  So it really comes down to supportable population.  And a -5 means you have probably just lost.  Build those farms!

RATING: 9.5
La Città has an interesting concept and game play. 
I enjoy it quite a lot. Building improvements for your cities are great. Will your city prosper or will you lose people to rival cities ?

I recently replayed this game. I had a wonderful time playing the game with the players I played with.

La Città takes a good 3-4 hours to play a full game with 5 players. It's the type of game which the 3-4 hours you spend are very enjoyable and doesn't feel like you play that long.

Unlike some other games which I played before which were long and boring...and made me want to leave the table yelling like an insane madman.

The time spent playing this little gem is well worth it.

The last time La Città was played, the game went very fast. We were quite surprised at the rate we were playing the game. We played probably our fastest game of La Citta ever. We enjoyed every minute of it. Of course it was with 5 experience players so there wasn't alot of dead time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Wow, I really like this game!  After only one play, I'm ready to toss Agricola, Steam, Puerto Rico, and Citadels into the fire.  This game, I feel, has the best components of those games (tile laying, worker management, role selection, strategy) without feeling like a total rip-off. 

Players are Italian princes that are building cities on a map that loosely reminds me of Catan.  There are certain tiles you must place buildings next to in order to receive resources or be able to expand the population of your city.  Citizens are needed to occupy the various buildings in each city.  The hook to this game is the 'voice of the people' mechanic.  Cards are randomized at the beginning of the game and once a year you find out what the citizens desire to remain content with your city.  There are 3 categories; culture, education, and health.  If you don't have what they want, you get to sadly watch as the citizens in your town choose to relocate to a neighboring city owned by an opponent.

I'd like to play it again, in fact I'm insisting my gaming group tackle it next week :)  A 3 player game, with setup, took us close to 3 hours, but I hope to knock that down to the estimated playtime of 100 minutes soon enough.  

Update:  We were able to give the game another go this past weekend.  One member of my group feared the game would have little to no replay value and he was terribly wrong.  I do wish we could try it 4p instead of just 3, so we would have access to the whole board.  It's very, very tough to get a food engine going if the better food tiles are randomized to sections of the board that can't be used.  Even with that hindrance, I still had a fantastic time playing the game.

RATING: 6
[2 plays] There are some novel aspects of this game that I like.  I liked the voice of the people, and the fact that La Città is like many games in being about competing civilizations (cities, nation-states, city-states, whatever), but unlike most of them in that it's not done through military conflict.  For once you don't arm up and go conquer your neighbors; you just make your city-state so fabulous that all their people want to emigrate.  It can still get pretty vicious, though.  And you can spend most of the game ironically poised between the Scylla of having your people emigrate, so that you have to destroy buildings, and the Charybdis of having new people immigrate whom you won’t be able to feed.  There’s also the fact that if you don’t know what the voice of the people will be, you may not know what to do, but if you spend actions on the voice of the people, then you’ll know what you want to do but won’t have the resources left to do it.  The city placement and growth have a Catan sort of feel, and like Settlers of Catan, initial placements are wildly important.

Well, I only have played once, with two, and that was my experience anyway as the unfortunate straggler.  I had one of those pathetic cities that acts as nothing more than a feeder of its own population growth to a neighboring metrolith, turn after turn.  

The migration mechanic is really excellent, but it feels like it wants to be part of a bigger, longer game (and perhaps that game is Antiquity, we'll see one day).  The mathematically interesting but thematically simple Voice of the People makes me long for a game that gave the topic a more loving treatment, including refugees from sustained war.  And I agree with whoever said that it would be nice to have individually colored citizens so you could see how the migrations occurred.

It satisfies more as a typical Euro thinker, rather than as some sort of Civ-lite.  The commonly cited design issues are true, but are only marginally distracting.

RATING: 5
This is one of those near-miss games that could've been a real winner were it not for a few fatal flaws. The biggest of these is the card track gets clogged with less than desirable cards and the last thing you want to do is take a mildly helpful card only to reveal a truly useful card for someone else. Secondly, as the board fills up, the calculations of which citizens will to which cities forces players to slow down and consider all angles which bogs the game down in AP and agonizingly slow turns. What's worse is that you don't feel an arc to this "calculation management" but rather a sense of doing the same thing over and over. 

Overall, it chugs down the runway but doesn't achieve lift off. Really wanted it to work, and I heard others with the same complaints had ideas for variants, but I traded mine.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Players: 2-5
Duration: 3 hrs.
Type: strategy, city building, worker management, area control, set collection

Like: Nice insert designed for pieces. The board setup can change from game to game, with advanced rules which engenders replay-ability. Rules clearly written. the People's defection/relocation mechanic, very clever--one I have not seen before. A player has to be very careful of its use because a defection may cause a loss of action for the player who built the more attractive city. 

Dislike: Small fiddly pieces; Setup extremely fiddly and time consuming; It also takes patience putting away; I find the color scheme hideous; Cards are small and hard to shuffle. These numerous reasons have made it very difficult to get it to the table--other games are just more inviting. Only repeated encouragement from others, especially Iron Gamer, has finally brought it out again. I believe the Hospital to be an over powering building which can only be built with a card--therefore, a strong luck feature of the game when that card appears. Long with a lot of player downtime because planning ahead is not always possible, and recalculating necessary. 

Tough decisions and a lot to keep track of. To help keep track, and reduce time spent by repeatedly adding up advantages, people, and food before each turn and at the end of each year, found this score-keeping file here on BGG, Citta Ref.doc, by txaal, essential (going to laminate & use wipe-able markers).

The unusual mechanic gives a nice 7 score to this game. I didn't rate it higher due to the length (3 hours min. at best) and because a lot of time is devoted to rechecking & recalculating even with the use of score keeping aids. In addition it is just fiddlier than I like, and playing mistakes are easy to make. Still it is a strong game with hard decisions, and many possible decisions so that players often make surprising moves.

Due to the length it is going to be harder to get this to the table, but am looking forward to more plays.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[City Building, Population Management, Resource Management, Planning, Card Drafting] [5P:6] First off, this is not a "Civilization game" in the Sid Meier sense of the word. La Citta is about supporting your citizens and growing your population - everything else is incidental. Your buildings are simply a means to an end - they don't contribute to your score at all. It's all about the people, which is pretty strange for a game where you spend a lot of time planning what to build. The rest of the time you're planning on how to feed your people. The theme works, but I'm not all that sold on the mechanisms. Kosmos has never been the development powerhouse that HiG or alea/Ravensburger is, so La Citta doesn't really flow as well as other Eurogames of similar complexity. Think Power Grid more than Puerto Rico. Also I realize that the board is already fairly large, but the physical game design leaves something to be desired. The food markers are not an ideal tracking mechanism, and having to recount your citizens constantly slows the game down. There's a lot to talk about, particularly in whether the game HAS to go 6 rounds and last 2-3 hours, but in the end La Citta seems to fall into the "good not great" light-mediumweight German game category.

Recommended by my Geekbuddies (7.76, 16 ratings) [Received the German Kosmos Big Box Edition in trade from Mr. Wilson "silverthorn 11" Tan, to whom I am most thankful.]

RATING: 6
In La Città, players build cities in order to attract as many citizens as possible. At the end of each turn, citizens move from their current place of living to any neighbouring cities that match their preferences better.

This directly leads up to the biggest issue I have with the game. While both thematically and mechanically, La Città is a fantastic game, unhindered migration results in the big getting bigger and the small getting smaller. Since there is nothing to dampen this effect the game effectively has player elimination: Once you've fallen behind a bit too much there is absolutely no way for you to come back. Part of the appeal is to not let this happen, of course, but there is only so much you can do.

I would certainly like to play this every once in a while, but I'm afraid I had much bigger hopes.

RATING: 7
Requires the same kind of thinking as the original Civilization (the computer game). There is, however, no computer to do the math for you, so half of your time is spent counting and re-counting - a serious drawback as it can really slow the game down. On the other hand, as far as I'm concerned, Civ was and is the best computer game out there, and this was the best boardgame spin-off I had seen before Through the Ages came along (although these two games are very different and focus on different aspects of the computer game - on on territory, the other on culture). The same turn angst applies where you would need to do several things at the same time, but the resources available are very limited (and they all boil down to our one ultimate resource, time). This game, however, does not get off balance after a while, like the computer game does (and its official boardgame version, according to most opinions). Luck of the draw plays a serious part, but as in all good games, this risk can be managed by careful players.

RATING: 6
Strategy lovers look no further. If you're a Caylus, Power Grid, or Puerto Rico fan, this one is for you. A classic Euro (with a particularly dry theme to match) which emphasizes decision-making and long-term planning above all else. One really has to weigh their decisions due to the limited number of actions per turn. This makes for thoughtful, and interesting game-play, but it can also lead to analysis paralysis in some.

The board scales for the number of players, so it's enjoyable, and competitive no matter how many you play with.

But, I did find it to be a bit boring and dull, as did the Lady. Although, I'm not one for heavy-thinking, or heavy gameplay, and neither is the Lady. We both prefer a bit more fast-paced games, with a smidge of luck. We're not much for brain-drainers, and this is one for certain.

If you like a thinking man's game, then pick up La Citta.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
La Citta is very cool, ingeniously-designed game whose reputation seems to have grown significantly since its release several years ago.  It's definitely cut from the classic German cloth, with streamlined mechanisms and tightly constrained choices pushing the game to its conclusion.  However, La Citta smoothly incorporates a lot of simulation-like elements, giving it a thematic richness that few German games have been able to achieve.

To win, you need to attract new citizens to your cities.  If your city is meeting its citizens' needs, people will actually immigrate from neighboring cities!  But you'll need to develop enough farmland to feed these people.  You'll need marketplaces and sufficient fresh water as your cities grow larger.  And where will you get the money to pay for all these civic projects?  I hope you've situated one of your cities where it can mine those valuable metals!  This all sounds a lot more like a computer game than a typical "Euro."  Yet all those elements are packed into a sleek and clever Euro game system without all the complications and exceptions and irregularities that one might expect to encounter in a simulation as flavorful as this.

RATING: 8.2
Rating after three plays with 5 players. Great game for strategics and tactics, agonizing decisions. Just the same some luck issues with what politics card is revealed by the player before you. The mechanics are greatly balanced but not so the victory points. The only victory points are given at the end of the game so you don't have actually an overview during the game who's first. Another issue is, in our 5player game the final results where within only 6 points spread (33p-27p). I would prefer greater gaps between players to feel that I won because my strategy was best and not I lost because I mixed up two buildings (ie. not getting that 3p bonus for three colors in a city which could be decisive). 

A comment on other commments: For all those who rated that game low because it's to longish or has fiddly bits or a 5-player game is to tenseful: you are whinies!

RATING: 8
Played 5+  There is a settlers vibe about this game. Unlike Settlers there is no robber to hold back the leader which can make for a "rich get richer" situation. This game has a lovely feel. Don't be fooled by the passive theme, this is one tense game. You go to "war" with your opponents by attracting citizens from their cities to yours by building facilities that they desire. Upon analyses after a game the losers can be left feeling they lost it after the first turn but so what, the experience is delightfully all consuming. *UPDATE* Just played a great 4 player game, I think it might be at its best with this number though I have not played with 5 yet. I am bumping this up to a 9. Feb 07

*UPDATE* I have passed on the opportunity to buy my own copy of this game for £10. This caused me to reflect on how I feel and I have dropped my rating by a point. It is my kind of game, I enjoy the theme and the advance Settlers vibe but one player will normally take a miserable beating which is not a great feature of a game. If I bring it to the table I'll be taking time away from newer and more worthy games. June 14

RATING: 7
There's a great game lurking in here, but I think it needs a few tweaks to bring it out.

Notably, I think the order of the Migration/Feeding phases should be reversed. The food/population balance in La Citta is like walking a knife's edge -- one slip can result in catastrophe. Losing even one too many citizens can cause a death spiral if it results in losing buildings as well. By itself this is not a problem. But since emigration happens *before* you feed the people, you need to recalculate your plans every time your opponents build anything. And this recalculation is not easy so it slows the game to a crawl.

By feeding the people first, you can plan your entire turn (somewhat) in advance, and react to any unforeseen migration problems from the previous turn. You still need to plan, you still need to watch your neighbours, but not to the extent that you must "re-work the numbers" every time a building is constructed. Given the amount of calculation and consideration still present (La Citta is a very meaty game), this is a welcome change.

RATING: 10
This is a GREAT game. A perfect "civ" game that plays in 2-3 hours. Novel mechanics capture the feel of building cities, much like the computer game, SimCity, but you're competing against each other!

Although it seems like a civ game on the outside, the game quickly becomes chess-like, with life-or-death moves on every turn. Your cities grow or shrink, depending on how well you've built your infrastructure--health, education, or cultural services--compared with your neighboring cities. Over six years/turns, you can watch your cities blossom into overflowing metropolises, or watch them die a slow, festering death, as the people get "sucked" to "better" cities.

It's immersive, putting almost complete control of your "empire" (well, not really, it's just two to four cities) in your hands. Are you a good planner? Manager? Visionary? It takes all that to overcome the seemingly random, and chaotic nature of the Voice of the People, which determines what services the people want, and if neighboring citizens will come to your city or your own will flee to greener pastures. The Hand of Fate is in every play, as what you can or cannot build will be dicatated by the random Political action cards, but you can always fall back on default actions.

The drawback is the time commitment. 20-25 minutes to explain the rules. 3-3.5 hours for a 4-5p game. May get shorter as players in my group get familiar with the game. The game is fiddly in the set-up and take-down: there are lots of tiles and citizen figures. Because of this and the slightly excessive playing time, I give this a '9', even though I really want to give this a '10'. Only time will tell if this becomes a frequent favorite.

The components are fabulous, the citizen figures are small, but detailed, and the rules are very well written. I can see why this is ranked in the 100's, but one would think such novel and challenging gameplay wouldn't find itself in the top 50.

Okay, the game is a brain-burner for AP-types, much like Power Grid. It is somewhat forgiving, but the results from your mistakes will be telling from the early get-go. It seems as painful as Age of Steam, yet I like this game: it is not a spreadsheet like AOS seems to be. My winning or losing isn't kept track on some linear VP track; I literally watch the rise and fall of my cities, over a period of time, where the citizen figures are my VPs! I cheer when my cities grow. I am in anguish and feel like a failure when my people leave. There's plenty of uncertainty in the cards, but isn't that what being a politician is all about? Give the people what they want! And soon! And more! There's never enough! I need more time! The tension, coupled with the uncertainty, with the stunning visual appeal, makes this a winner. Someone commented that this feels more like a wargame than a civ-game, since there are area control and front lines. The feeling of winning/losing citizens is more like warfare than an exercise in civic planning.

Update (09/12/09) 5P game: 25 minutes to explain. 3 hrs 15 minutes playing time with moderate AP play near the end. This is not a game to bring to a boardgame meetup; one needs to dedicate a good chunk of an gaming evening to get this to the table, where players want a longer, meatier game--this game delivers that in spades. It seems starting a third city is essential to winning. Plenty of farms is necessary, while quarries are not--they're nice,  one or two is probably a good idea; three or four can be devastating, if the city(s) can survive the initial "attacks" from surrounding neighbors. In a fixed setup, 2-3 quarries is possible, but in a random setup, 4 gold quarries is a recipe for player collusion to take you out.

Update (09/16/09) 4P game: 20 minutes to explain to one person. 10 min setup. 2.5 hrs play. 10 minutes to put away. The 4P seems to be the sweet spot: enough space to expand and build with little conflict that is present in turn 1 of a 5P "random" setup game, and the tempo is just right. Introducing one or two newbies is the way to get a foothold on the table. One-two game veterans love this game--I love this game! However, the 4P has a probable weakness--one player will likely set up a city far away from everyone else in the "desolated" part of the board (i.e. one farm spot, otherwise rocks and lakes), and run away with the game, unless others move in too. 5P is crowded and cut-throat, but the game is designed for that number. 4P is more relaxed and leisurely in comparison. It depends one what the players want. I'm upping this to a '10'. A solid, deservedly "classic" game. If the English version of this had come out sooner, this surely would have been up there with Power Grid. 

Update (09/20/09) 4P game: 2 hrs 20 min with all veterans, and board previously setup. Gameplay is getting quicker with experience. New strategies are emerging. Original player aids are generally worthless. The new ones available are okay, but all are disconnected and don't have all the info. New player aid is definitely needed for newbies due to all the symbology, as there are no text anywhere! However, it is all intuitive after several plays. City tiles need to be sorted into trays, using a solution similar to Le Havre.

Update (10/14/10) 8 plays: first 3P game, took 2.5 hours. The feel of the game changes, where farms become far more  important than quarries (and money), since too much population growth is now the problem and not the other way around. All in all, a very close game: 34, 33, and 31 pts respectively.

RATING: 6.5
2015-05-31

Initial Rating: 6.5 (May 2015)

I didn’t have any expectation whatsoever, but I found a good game in La Città. The game looks gorgeous on the table, the components are very good too. The rules are easy to grasp, but the decisions needed in the game are very interesting and meaty. You have only 5 actions each turn and you have lots of options. The luck of the draw isn’t small, but I feel it doesn’t decide the fate of the game (most of the time).

An early mistake can take you out of the game for good, in general I like this kind game (Age of Steam, Antiquity, Myrmes) BUT in this case I don´t because sometimes you have to take decisions with incomplete information. That’s not the part I dislike (in fact I love that) because there is a mechanism to control it (a card that allows you to poll the voice of the people). What really bugs me is when you spend a costly action to look 2 of the voice of the people cards and that gives you no valuable information whatsoever (when the face up card is black, and you looked at a white card and at a blue card). I realize this has a low probability of happening, but in our game it happened twice, and one of the players guessed wrong so he finished last. In conclusion, I’m ok to losing the game in the first or second turn if it was my fault, but I’m not ok if I have to guess a path EVEN after I paid for a card so I didn’t have to guess. I also think this can be mitigated paying attention to what the other players cities are building. I need to play more times to see if this is really an issue or not.

La Città also has a “the richer get richer” feel because when you steal citizen you grow stronger and you maintain that strength while at the same time weakening your competitors, in this way you can achieve an unrivaled advantage. In out game there were 2 super cities and all others lost to them. These cities won from round one. I’m not sure if this is an issue for me, but I think not. Perhaps it was due to our inexperience, as I said before I need to play again.
 
The insert looks great, but it is useless because when you transport the game, all bits become disorganized :angry:, I will toss it and use bags instead.

I thought the “feed your workers” mechanism was invented around 2007 (Agricola) but this game is from 2000 and it causes a lot of tension, I love it.

Bottom line, I want to play again in the near future and I will keep the game in my collection. La Città has a lot of potential and perhaps it will get an upgraded rating. I feel I has lots of depth and I would like to explore it.

RATING: 7
A very clever design with some unique elements. The 'Voice of the People' scoring and the related emigration rules in particular provide a unique take on conflict / player interaction. (It is very interactive for a Euro, which I like).

It can be quite brutal - if you get hosed early on by the Voice of the People it's a long road back. Not saying that's a bad thing, but it is something to be aware of. Even winning these conflicts can be a problem if you don't have enough food to feed the people you leech off the opposition - 'build another farm' is rarely a bad idea.

It looks nice, but some of the colour / design choices could have been better. The yellow player castles are almost the same colour as the building tiles. Having the arches aligned the same way across all tiles played helps when parsing the board.

All in all, could be the perfect Euro for wargamers!

RATING: 6
La Citta is more about population control than city building. Case in point, your city cannot grow without citizens and your city collapses when citizens leave.

The buildings themselves are really of little consequence as both large and small arch buildings are worth the same at games end. The larger buildings will help you attract citizens during the game but then you may not be able to feed them which will in turn cause those citizens to leave thereby destroying your city.

This concept is really cool and should fill each session with tension and brain melting goodness; unfortunately my sessions have been undone by monstrous AP and downtime, sucking out all the tension in the game. The sessions end up being way too long for the game's depth.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
La Città is a game that pulls a bait-and-switch... a really, really good one.

At first glance the game appears to be about farming, mining, and building. Those may be the fundamentals of the game, but on closer inspection they exist to enable a much more interesting concept: the ebb and flow of population. Each turn, the "voice of the people" cries out for better education, culture, or public health, and cities will flourish or wither depending on how they answer the call. In this way, players compete directly with each other, trying to siphon off population, perhaps sending a once-proud city into ruin and signaling the rise of a burgeoning capital. Growth is not always good, though. If a city cannot support it's people via sufficient farmland, it may be desirable to lose a competition with another city. In fact, even if a city can sustain more growth, allowing population to migrate to a struggling neighbor becomes a form of attack, inflicting starvation and ruin. This is why La Citta works. Its three basic elements - upkeep (farming), currency (mining) and hidden information (voice of the people) - create a system in which cities function as magnets. Managing their relative force is the game.

Unfortunately, there does seem to be a glitch or two in the mechanics. In our game, the people wanted culture early on, enough so that players knew it would never be valued again in the last 2/3 of the game. But, it was only after they wanted culture that the option to build culture-specific buildings (cathedrals etc) came up. Essentially, the action-menu (7 cards) became clogged with actions no-one would ever take, drastically reducing options and increasing luck. Not cool.

So, while I thoroughly enjoyed the concept of La Citta, I'm not sure how often the game glitches out. Proceed with caution.

RATING: 7
A thinking man's game, where balance and timing is everything.. 
Get either wrong and you may fail miserably - falling behind in the process.
Manage the challenges well and watch your cities thrive and blossom.
The tension is palpable and the planning crucial, which does make it catching.
Think CIV building game, without armies and wars or conquests, yet it elegantly 
creates fierce competition but also some animosity between the players.

Ooh, I do want to give it 8, but the game mechanism are very static and slightly bothersome, and you do have a feeling of being a spectator on occasion.
Early "wipe-outs" are severely difficult to recover from, runaway leaders are hard to catch. Lacks room for creative strategies, too - and your true options are limited.
Beware of "Analysis Paralysis" in this one..

RATING: 7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Scales well from 2-5, though it is more chaotic with more since you have more interactions and more card turnover.

At the end of each of the 6 turns, you defend your city from influence with 1 (occastionally 2) of 3 colors you must add to your city determined by the VOICE OF THE PEOPLE.  These are represented by 4 cards, only one is face up.  This aspect is the only imperfect knowledge of the game, but can be mitigated by using an action to peak at the face down cards.

Scoring is very simple, 1 point per citizen, and 3 for each "well developed" city (meaning has atleast 1 of each 3 colors).  The real trick is finding enough farm space to feed a high population count late in the game.

My main complaint is in the latter rounds (say the last 2 of the 6) as the board gets hard to distinguish since for each city only a single tile represents player color, and all citizens and city additions are generic in color.
Also, it starts taking a while to add up arches, # of citizens, and if you have enough farms to feed them.

RATING: 9
A lot of fun, and simpler to play than it looks (each round has a rhythym to it that you can pick up quickly). I need a better grasp of strategy, and am apt to rely on the crutch that your short-term actions are dictated by which Political Cards are available. That scoring only occurs at the very end makes it hard for me to weigh my performance midgame. Works equally well for 2 or 5; I have only played it s a 3-player once, and have a vague recollection that one player got squeezed between the other two. The tension between wanting to attract nobles and to avoid the -5 starvation in the last round can turn into a seriously delicious tension. Does this really need a Renaissance Italy theme? It is a theme I enjoy, but this game could have any kind of historical or fictional setting, and placing it in the Renaissance when so many other games are seems gratuitous. I think I'd rather we were building up little Fertile Crescent city-states, and maybe instead of the 3 services, each could be represented by a different ancient god to whom your city could devote temples.

RATING: 7
As much as such a thing is possible, this feels like somewhat of a crossover between Settlers of Catan and Tigris & Euphrates. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Fortunately, most elements of the game seems to be logical and make sense which is surprising compared to many euro games that use weird illogical mechanics.

But the reason why I drop the rating to 4 is that there are various annoying stuff in the game which drops seriously my interest.

There are various flaws in the physical design of the game. First, the buildings are all the same color, so it gets hard to identify which building is what. They should have clearly identified each building so that at glance, you know if you are missing a special building or not. 

Second, the player pawns are similar to the population piece and placing the pawn on the side of the arch on the tile is very confusing. They should have given 3 colored tokens: black, blue and white. This way, the analysis would have been easier.

Third, since the first game you play you should start with a fixed setup, they should have made sure that the information was written directly on the board for the city location and the resources. Then you would have used the resource triangles if you want to change them.

Fourth, it is not indicated on the fountain that it can make you grow more than 8 population.

Now about the rules of the game:

First, the map design is bad. There is no resources on the side of the board, so expanding or starting new cities there is totally useless.

Second, you can start additional cities but there are few good spot to actually start new cities since like I said before, the sides of the board are worthless. I thought that with less player you would build more cities, but that is not the case since with less players you do not use all the board.

Third, the fountain had the problem that if you forget to build a fountain, your city gets locked. The only way to grow is to lose some population, grow again and build a fountain. The fountain should have given you a free population like the market place did. Or allow you to place it without population.

Finally, the population movement make sense, but the analysis is so complicated especially when you are halfway through the game and when there is a tie in the colors. Part of this is because it is hard to know which city comparison has been done ( at least in china, each comparison is labeled) and second because of the poor component design explained above makes it harder to analyze.

So in overall, it could have been an acceptable game, but in the end it is not that good.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a thoughtful-population planning tile-laying and growth/control game that requires careful attention to citizen migrations between all players' cities.  I suspect that being the central player on the map board raises the highest potential risk unless it is a powerful city.  Turn order is important as it seems mostly better to go last.  IMO it would be more balanced if all players move citizens and then limits are imposed in finality of that phase.  Overall, I think it is a heavy game but lumbersome (over 2.5 hrs) with a good deal of long-term nuances to think about.  Might possibly be best with 5 players or 3, but probably not 4 b/c of how the map is adjusted (no modification for 4?, hmmm...).

RATING: 5
I like this game, but feel that there's a bit too much complexity and fiddliness that it not matched by either its depth or the fun it brings.

We perceived some runaway leader problems on the first play.  When a player gets a big city going, it's so hard to stop it pulling away citizens from its neighbors, creating a feedback loop where big cities get bigger and smaller ones stay stagnant or get smaller.

However, it's a high-information game so you can see this coming.  It just requires players who can see this developing and take actions to stop it.  This would not have been obvious on the first play.

Game needs some kind of citizen markers to go with the food markers so one doesn't have to keep counting the figurines and comparing against food.  Homebrew this.

This game can EASILY be analysis-paralyzed to death.  It's a busy board but the options are not THAT vast,so PLEASE, when playing, plan your move during the other players' turns and take it in 30 seconds when the move comes to you.  This game should not take more than 2 hours to play.

Board looks great at the end... the theme is sold and I do feel as if I've presided over an expansion of city-states in the Italian countryside.  I would give this another spin, but the vast bookkeeping in the game convinced me to trade it away, so I don't see myself playing it in the near future, if ever.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
After one play with 4-players I am mostly impressed. The rules are fairly easy to grasp and the gameplay rewards strategic over tactical play. In some ways, the game reminds me of Agricola (I've only played the solo flash version). In both games the players have to worry about feeding their citizens/families. The similarities probably end there, but the feeling was the same regardless.

Although I think this is a solid game, it is not without drawbacks. Specifically the icons bother me. The most important aspect on the hexes you play are the tiniest icons (arches, ">5", etc.). Now take these small icons then place citizens on them to make them even more difficult to see. Both of these aspects make it difficult to assess the state of the board. This is bothersome as there is a lot going on without the components getting in the way.

To be fair, these downsides are not very bad but they do make me wonder. The game relies on many interacting mechanisms to work well. There are so many mechanisms that it is quite possible to be overcomed by them all. With the questionable usability of the components, are mechanisms manageable or is this game merely glorified chaos? Only future plays will answer this question.

RATING: 7
A clever area control game where you grow cities by acquiring tiles.  The tiles help satisfy a changing demand for services or provide food or money.  Cities that get big and get close to other players can may then take citizens from other players cities.  

Citizens count toward victory points at the end of the game.  

This is a game that rewards multiple plays.  If you start poorly, or make a error early, you will not recover.  

Gamers game not a family game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[Played about 10 times]
Strategy game, with some luck and a lot of planning. Scales well. Nice bits.
Its flaws are:
I) the cards aren't balanced and so sometimes you buy a card and the card that is revealed to replace it (and thus is avaible for your opponent) is much better than all the 7 you had available.
II) the "Voice of the People" is utterly random. Once in a while you can mitigate it with a "Polling the People", even if there's nothing worse than a Poll (not paying the extra 2$) revealing 3 different cards... and I'm inclined to believe that 2$ and an action is a bit overpriced for the full effect, especially since the other players can simply "copy" your next moves. Bluffing won't help unless you're the last player and can thus do some "surprise" action in the last turn; but if you go last will all the other players ignore that "Polling the People"?
III) the "3 food" tile is overpowered. If it comes near a "2 food" tile, whoever goes first during the setup has got a great advantage with no drawback. This is very bad for a "strategy game".
IV) once a city is "doomed" (at least two neighbouring cities "stronger" than it), there's no hope to save it. Losing persons means losing builings, that means losing more persons during later turns and so on. This can easily happen in the first half of the game (and having to play three other turns with no hope to win can be acceptable for a 30-minutes card game, but not in a 2-hour strategy game!).

RATING: 5
Interesting conception, but playing wasn't fun.

The reasons are:
1. It doesn't have so much civilization-building feel. All the tiles seems similar, there is little freedom in laying tiles, your city grows weird linear shape.

2. You don't have much choice. What you should do is relatively obvious.

3. Your possibilities of choice depends on opened cards. If cards allows you to do what you should do, you can do it. Otherwise, you cannot.

It is regarded as a kind of civ game, but I think it as middle-weight tile laying game, wanting to be a civ game. Important charmings of civ games have been cut down. In addition, it is long for what it is.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Game in a Nutshell:  Players compete for space and population by expanding their cities so they can score points for having well rounded services to support as many citizens as they can get.

Played as:  3,4 players (preset boards), 3 player (random board)

Pros:  I love the balance between the goal of the game, which is to get lots of citizens, and how too many citizens can actually hurt you if your cities aren’t ready to support them.  It’s a twisted sort of fun to watch players get excited to steal a bunch of people from neighboring cities with their sweet health services only to later realize they don’t have the food to support their increased population.  All this is a result of a need for some perfectly blended strategy and tactics.  The interaction is direct but light enough to not offend easily.  I’m not sure why, but for some reason it’s easy for me to feel like I’m on the ground level with this map.  The Modular placement of the tiles on initial set-up really works since the map aspect feels more intimate. It’s a nice step up the Civ-building ladder from Settlers.

Cons:  Even though it works perfect thematically, I think the way it requires players to look ahead and plan for expansion may be a turn off for some.  You’re really playing now for what you need five moves in the future, so if you’re playing for immediate needs then chances are, you’re already behind the ball.  What I’m trying to say is there’s a level of concentration and planning needed that might make for a less enjoyable experience for some.  Some small niggly details that bother me: fiddly pieces (and lots of them), and at times it can be tough to differentiate your city from someone else’s.

Other:  I can’t help but think of this game as the best alternative for Settlers of Catan fans wanting to pick up an expansion.  Because of that, I think this game is undervalued and deserves more attention then it gets. Much tighter game as the number of players increase.

Best Use:  A true next step after Settlers or just a solid light-civ Euro with some good interaction and well implemented theme.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Longish; vicious.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
La Citta is a great game. I feel it gets some harsh comments because it is quite a bit heavier than it is made out to be: it is, or was, portrayed as "one of those" building-buying medium weight type of games, but is significantly more complex and unforgiving than any of those that might come to mind. La Citta scales remarkably well, but is probably the most fun experience with 5. However, it is also the most complicated with 5, as interactions and intertwining decision trees become increasingly more complex. It is impossible to play La Citta lightly, or to approach it "intuitively" based on the flow of the game. Repeated counting of your civilians is mandatory, combined with your exact estimates on possibilities for that number after migrations of the turn. Should you be off by one, chances are you get punished by one less action or 5 points deduction - both huge penalties. (I feel that each player should have had some aid to keep track of their crop+citizens in an easier manner to shorten the endless counting.)
In addition, early building purchases have a big impact on your further game, so planning is needed. 
But, once you get the hang of La Citta, it is a wonderful game, with excitement to the end. People who are whining about runaway leader problems are not playing the game properly: if something goes wrong early in the game with one of your cities, damage control is the key in this game, not: "oh, I'll try elsewhere". Letting go of something you've invested in is not an option.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Players to attempt to build cities which will be influential enough to attract people from competing player cities, thus allowing them to end with the most people of any player.  The board is randomly created, and then players must place cities so that they are able to get food, gold, and access water (all of which are required to expand the cities and build other things that make the city desirable to people).  The game is very competitive, as players will necessarily end up trying to steal people from other players' cities.  Cities compete for people based on one of three types of desirable characteristics (obtained by building specific buildings), and it's only partially known which characteristic will be used in each turn to determine which city is the most desirable.

Overall this is an enjoyable, thought requiring game, but I wish that there were more mechanisms to allow large buildings to be built (as large buildings require specific cards to be built, and the cards available often stagnate because players that I play with often don't choose cards, meaning that there is at most 1 useful building card available).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3
This is a very interesting game that probably could have been a classic had it been tightened up a little. Players are challenged to balance a number of factors as they start and expand cities. If you make your city attractive, you'll steal population (which double as VPs) from other players, but if you don't have enough food to feed your new residents, you get burned. Furthermore, you're making bets on what "the people" want; bet wrong and you'll be the one sending people to others' cities. Resource management, spatial placement, and timing all come into play.

However, I can see where the complaints about this title are coming from. Your actions are largely constrained by the cards available in the card display, which got clogged in our game (and apparently many others) by unappealing cards that never get removed. This results in the face down cards having more perceived value than anything that's available, so players don't want to use weak cards for fear that an awesome card will get revealed for the next player, further clogging the card display. This also gives a slight advantage to the start player, who will probably have a display full of decent cards to pick from.

In addition, the game feels like it runs just a little too long. Turns feel redundant, especially as actions become extremely constrained toward the end. Whatever arc there is to the game could probably have been condensed into something closer to an hour, perhaps by more aggressively changing the board per player and chopping a couple rounds off.

In the end, this feels like a game with great potential but misses the mark. I might increase my rating with some tweaks, like [thread=340181]flushing the action cards[/thread] and/or chopping a round off the game. Even more interesting might be a card drafting variant, by which players start the game by selecting cards from the deck that they will play over the course of the game, but that's probably getting a little crazy.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

4 (2-5) 2-5

RATING: 6
La Città is difficult to judge. On one hand, there are civilization aspects that I like, such as the constant struggle for food, money and development, and I like how the rule that people move to cities where "the grass is greener" creates tension between the players. On the other hand, those very restrictions sometimes makes you feel that the game plays you, leaving little room for creative strategies. Perhaps the essence of the game is the small subtle decisions but in that case I need to play more to understand them.

Update: Sold because I felt that the People's Voice was too unpredictable to reward strategic play and the remaining decisions are simply not enough to warrant such a long game.

RATING: 7
A fascinating way to tie in area majority control with geometrical control, and my rating might well increase after a few plays.

Daft colour scheme doesn't really help, but the little men are a delightful addition and there is almost a card-game-like quality in the way the distribution of the politics cards decides the pace of the game. As has since become a commonplace idea, having not enough actions to do everything leads to some agonising decisions.

The starting position of the 3-food tile needs house-ruling to prevent blowouts. Especially in 3P.

RATING: 7
規則:
*建築版塊分類疊起，有兩面的盡量平均分配(一種不夠用可拿另外一個翻面使用)，地形版塊隨機放置圖板上(第一次完有建議配置)，政策卡32張洗勻放圖版對應位置該七張，27張人民之聲卡洗勻蓋著放對應位置，開一張另外旁邊蓋著放三張
*每人選一個顏色拿4起始城堡版塊跟4個同色小人還有一組三張行動卡，可照建議設置放起始城市，或是隨機起，正序反序各放一次，總共每人放兩個起始城市，每個城市上面放三個人，根據相連農田拿對應食物標記，之後起始是第一個放城市的左邊玩家
*遊戲進行六輪(用使用過的人民之聲卡紀錄)，每輪有八個階段
1.起始玩家左傳，第一輪不做
2.放置人民之聲卡(同第一輪一樣一張開三張蓋)
3.每個相鄰山脈的礦場，獲得同等山脈上標示數量的金幣(相鄰兩個都會拿)
4.每個城市城堡版塊放置一個小黑人，(如果有上限不能超過)
5.政治階段 照順位每人輪流執行一個行動，總共五輪，每次執行完的牌會在面有蓋著紀錄，可執行政治牌列(馬上補新的)的牌或是手上三張(如果沒行動可做，從政治牌頂牌蓋著放面前代表跳過)
>拿兩元
>放置城堡，跟其他城市至少一格距離，根據相鄰農田拿食物標記，移動一個你自己城市的人到上面在額外放兩個上去
>蓋最小的房子  農田拿相鄰的食物標記，市集可讓人口上限超過5，噴泉可讓人口上限超過8，但是要跟水相鄰，其他會有三種需求
*蓋建築時只要跟一座城市相鄰即可，要馬上移動城堡的人到上面(城堡還是要留1)，且放置的位置不能造成兩座城市相連
>政治卡蓋建築 要支付錢，公共澡堂還有噴泉的能力
***特殊政治卡***
>麵包和遊戲  支付0/2/5圓放1~3個彩色小人到一個建築物的一個指數上去，此回合該指數+1
>黃金時間 支付0/2/5圓，放置1~3小人到城市城堡區，不能超過上限
>豐收  放置一個彩色小人到一農田上，該回合產量翻倍
>民調  不付錢看兩張蓋著的人民之聲卡，付兩元可全看
6.加總人民之聲  全部打開，需求最多的指數為這回合的需求，兩個平手則玩家可任選，每個城市可選不同
7.人口遷移 照順位，每個玩家一個一個結算自己的城市，比較此回合人民的需求指數，如果距離兩格內有對方城市指數比你低的，他要給你一個人口(每個不同玩家的每個城市都會算一次)，沒有人的版塊移除，不能因此拆成兩塊城市，在拆除前可移動人
8.每個玩家計算人口數，如果食物不足選不足的數量人口移除，且下輪要蓋掉一張行動卡，最多只能行動四次，最後一輪的話就扣分，最後一輪餵食不能使用政治卡的產量加倍
*輪結束，棄掉人民之聲卡，使用的政治卡放回棄牌區，行動卡翻面回手，有色小人都回手
*六輪結束每個小人一分，每個城市如果三種指數都有額外加三分，最後一次餵食如果餵不飽的扣五分
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I played this on a recent trip back to the UK while at Nick Fisk's (moviebuff) shop in Hanley, Shire Games. I liked it and didn't feel like waiting for an English reprint so I just bought the german version and Nick supplied the English rules and player aids!

The theme is reminiscent of Settlers in that players are trying to build up multiple cities while competing positionally for resources. That said, the mechanics and player interactions are completely different since instead of competing purely for  resources you're also attempting to improve your city so that the population from surrounding cities will want to move to yours. 

All in all a really fun game that my usual gaming group seem to be enjoying a lot as well so I think this will become a regular.

RATING: N/A
城市

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a good game. Your cities are always growing citizens, so you build new farms and buildings to feed 'em and entertain 'em. If you have more entertainment than other players' nearby cities, you steal their citizens. But this is only good if you can feed them with farms, which you may not have enough of if you've been building entertainment. Lots of good stuff to play with.

The downside is that it's hard to pull back once your on the slippery slope of despair, but there are interesting decisions, it plays well for 2 to 5 players (well, maybe 4 players) and the random setup creates a tense beginning and different challenges each game. We play the variant where we wipe all cards each round.

RATING: 8
Feels a lot like Sim City - 3 demand areas that each citizen wants.  Unlike Sim City though, these citizens are fickle, and want each one at different times.  It's important to do well in all three, since you'll need them all at some point, but each one has a certain time where it's more important.  City development brings some enjoyable decisions; should I get another city and more citizens, or just focus on the ones I have?  More players serve to make the competition a little more fierce, as players are forced to balance immigration a little more precisely to ensure adequate food sources.  A fun game that is prone to some Analysis Paralysis if you don't keep on top of slow people.

RATING: 7.3
1st play of this game with 4 players took 4 hours(!).  Quite a lot of AP, but I don't mind this too much.  The biggest flaw we found with this game is the random politics cards.  Some people would get a lucky one, whilst other unwanted ones sat and accumulated over time.  

I think if we play this again, we'll try a house variant to counter this.  Some possibilities are that at the end of a year, any duplicate politic cards are removed and replaced with a fresh draw.  -or-  Possibly limiting the deck to only having 2 of each type of card (a lot of reshuffles, but more guaranteed variety).

There were a few faults with some of the design choices, such as the market place blends in too much with other tiles meaning that players often forget they don't have one (this happened several times).

Despite the problems, I enjoyed the game and I'd definitely play it again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
This game is TOO unforgiving. Like Settlers of Catan, it suffers from the problem that an early mistake or poor luck can cripple a player for the rest of the game, relegating them to a distraction and source of citizens for other players. What compounds this problem is that the game is easily 2 hours long.

And the card luck is significant in La Citta. The Voice of the People can have a tremendous impact on two rival players, making the difference in direction of a 1-citizen swap (so +1 instead of -1, which is significant), and the available action cards are a source of irksome randomness as well. I do enjoy the theme and feeling of building up city states, but the game is too broken and luck-based to make it something I would regularly want to play.

RATING: 7.75
The game is actually less complicated than it looks and despite some fiddly stuff and not very elegant design (not the mechanics but the upkeep of available food, population limit etc.). I like that there are really not a lot of victory conditions like in many other games and even though there are always quite a few choices to be made, the focus never derails and the goal is clear. I wish the game would have more streamlined and you make a lot of mistakes just because there are so many things to keep track of and the game does not help you in doing it. The game is confrontational without being vicious because if you steal people, you have to feed them after all. Maybe it could have done with a bit more card variety and randomness, but that would probably made it much less of a euro.
