RATING: 7
November 2015: Down from 7 to 6.

Well-designed, elegant city building game. Very good strategic decisions: Do I start a third city? Which cities should I build up, and when? Turns move fast too.

Unfortunately, not that much fun. Although it's very confrontational, the interaction is limited, in the form of blocking and stealing citizens from players. Yes, the interaction is very significant, it just happens too infrequently.

Also, the tactical puzzles the game offers aren't that very interesting.

May 2007 (I think): Played this once two player and once five player. Preferred the five player version since it is very interactive and confrontational!

Very strategic: You only have thirty turns in the game. Very thematic as well; my gaming group has compared this game to Civilization (the computer game).

The biggest fault with this game is the length of time it takes to play it.

RATING: 5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
La Citta is beautiful and the mechanic of moving citizens from one city to another city that has better services is unique.  The common pool of politics cards creates a lot of tension by forcing me to wait until the other players pick actions to get to pick my own. 

La Citta's rules are fairly easy to learn for a civilization style game. I would say it plays pretty well with two people too.  The board is set up so that it scales well depending on the number of players.

It also has a lot of conflict and player interaction for a no battle Euro game.  Because each player is aiming to steal the citizens from their opponents' less successful cities, the conflict is very direct.  It surprises me that I like La Citta as much as I do because in most cases, the more direct the conflict, the more I dislike a game, but there is something fantastic about the delight of building up a city that cuts through my usual dislike of conflict.  It's fun to build sectors of the city near the food and the mountains that are the "working class" part of the city, while placing the public baths and statues and universities in a prettier area next to a lake.  (Okay, I guess that is just my peculiar fancy.)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
This is a very solid euro of the economic-development kind. It has a low level of player interaction (but is not multi-player solitaire) and a nice development arc. It feels about medium-weight to me (needs more brainpower than Ticket to Ride but less than Tikal or Age of Steam) but there may be depths I don't appreciate yet. The production quality is good, and the artwork is beautiful, detailed, and amazingly varied.

I usually avoid fractional ratings, or else I might give this a 7.5: it's better than many games I rate a 7, but seems to lack the spark that would make me give it an 8. There is tension, but it isn't unremitting; sometimes your best move is obvious. Also, it always helps a game to have a cool and memorable  "signature" mechanism, but La Città falls a bit short. I suppose its signature is the emigration of citizens (and therefore victory points) to the most attractive cities, and that's cool but not quite enough to make it stand out from other games of this kind. Yet La Città does have its own personality, and really is a good example of the genre.

If you like euros, you will probably greatly enjoy La Città.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Not only can players hurt one another's positions, doing so actually directly benefits them.

In many euros, you can hinder another player's access to resources, but, unless you're after those resources, yourself, you're sort of "falling on your sword" to inconvenience someone else. This can create situations where only those not involved in the conflict benefit.

This does not apply to La Citta, which the two resources over which you're fighting, food and population, both always help you and further your own goals.

RATING: 6
Interesting mechanics of managing city growth and migration of people to the more attractive cities, but in end the game kind of falls flat for me.  It just seems like an old Euro in feel, particularly in that points are only scored at the end - more modern games usually allow for scoring mid-game to make it more interesting, and to avoid having end scores very close.  Also, the game really needs ways to better track things: each player should have a player board, both to place your 5 cards per year as you take your turns (not a huge necessity), and with a population and food tracker (very necessary), so you aren't constantly adding up your population.  And it needs a more visual method for showing how many arches of each color each city has - it's too easy to miss an arch when you're scanning the board.

RATING: 7
It's taken a couple of games and I'm finally beginning to get the hang of it. Lots of choices and planning keeps you engaged even when it isn't your turn. Player interaction is a factor - don't want my people to leave town now. It must be near impossible to achieve the perfect game and therein lies a worthy challenge. I rate it just that little bit higher because it plays so well through the entire range of 2-5 players.

RATING: 8.5
A great game, but not for everyone. Gets better with every play. There's great tension, since you want to do so much more in a turn than is possible. Some people hate that, but the tension is the whole point of the fun! People who suck at it, end up hating it. Play it with a group who love games with some depth and richness. The production value is also top-notch. Plays great with two players as well, and the game is usually closer. The more cities on the board, the harder it might be too catch up if you're losing citizens left and right. Grossly underated, and deserves more attention than it gets.

RATING: 8
This is a favorite of mine and Grandpa's, but nobody else in the house likes it. Lots of tough decisions throughout and the resource and obligation juggling throughout the game really keep you on your toes.  <BR><BR>There are a couple of rules that are easy to miss on first reading, but the bits are excellent and the the rulebook is first rate. <BR><BR>The only fault that I find with the game is the persistence of Politics cards from one round to the next.  We tend to go with some house rules and generally decide ahead of time whether we will swap out one, all or none of the Politics cards at the end of each year.  This makes the game very tight or very loose, depending upon the card swap.<BR><BR>Beautiful layout when the game is in progress.  A great game!

RATING: 4
Big-box game that disappointed me, although the potential is great. I have serious issues with how at least one player will always be screwed after only a couple of turns. It sucks having to play one more hour (or more) with the certainty you're going to end dead last. This may be the player's fault, but the game makes sure that there will always be at least one such player.
In addition to that, there is a big difference between the power of the various cards. Some are always good, others are always (nearly) useless. If you get stuck with useless cards, you have to waste a turn with a bad card. Picking that card will usually reveal a good card for the next player. Cursing your luck is the only answer there, I'm afraid. For a game of this lenth and depth, I think that's a pretty bad feature.
All in all, not a game I would like to play again.

RATING: 8.5
Good theme play for a euro.  Easy to learn and play; however, it takes a few plays to pick-up the subtle nuances and strategies.  Game play can be tense, offers many choices, and has very good player interaction for a euro.  Game has a simple scoring system and scales very well.  Components are very good - colorful mounted board, ceramic citizen pieces, thick cardboard chits, durable small size cards.  Rulebook is good too, although could have been organized slightly better.  Player colors are not standard which is a minor niggle.  Game length is perfect, running about 90 minutes.  Rapidly becoming one of my favorites!

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[Kosmos 2000 edition] {Italy, civilization; tile-laying, resource management; optimal 4-5p} La Città is a solid and attractive strategy Euro game. It provides both tactical options and strategic planning with low luck. Players compete to have the largest population and finest cities in the region. It's fun, thematic and is full of interactions. It's multifaceted because players have several ways to increase the population of their cities.

Players have to plan carefully so that they can maintain the resources (food and gold) effectively and keep the growth of city and growth of population steady. Frequently, players may find that they can't expand a city due to limited population. Voice of people is growing important at the late game because migration of citizen is one crucial way to increase the population of a city. It is occasionally exciting to reveal the voice of people because it may force a player to over-populate.

In all, La Città is a good game for gamers. General gamers may find it's too deep for playing.

RATING: 7.4
I really enjoyed this game, despite its flaws. It's a bit fiddly to score, but not too bad if you're careful. Less forgiveable is that the random politics cards came out really badly in our first round, leaving none that were worth picking at all. This caused the selection of cards to become somewhat stale right from the outset. While I'm more than happy to give the game another chance, perhaps utilising a variant to ameliorate the issues, others in our group have been turned off the game entirely by this problem. That's a real shame, as it seems unlikely now that it will ever see the table again.

I'm willing to admit that, after only a single game, we might not have understood the cards well enough to declare them truly useless. But after a long discussion on the forum, no-one's managed to convince me that this is the case, and I can't help but see this as a disappointing flaw in an otherwise rather excellent game.

Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 1
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 1
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 4
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 1

RATING: 9
I really wish I had paid attention to the time when I started this game.  This is supposed to be a fairly long game, but it sure didn't feel like it.  It felt like it took about an hour, but I there's no way that the game was that fast.

Obviously I enjoyed my single play of the game.  You start out easy; the first turn doesn't seem to be likely to have alot of conflict unless you are really trying to get into someone's face.  After that, cities start to encrouch on each other and you have basics in place to start some competition.  The game gets tighter and tighter as it progresses.  You won't have enough of anything and if you do, it probably won't be true for long.

It's funny that my impression of the game seems to directly contradict some other people's complaints about the game.  I did not find it even slightly dry or overly complicated, altough I'm sure it helped that I had experianced players handling the rules.  There is certainly alot of calculation involved, and the penalty for mistakes can be high, so I can understand those objections.

RATING: 4
La Citta reminds me that when we rate games after only a play or two we can never seperate the game itself from our total experience, even though that experience is affected by so much more than the game. How the game is taught, the environment we are playing in and the group we are playing with all contribute to the feeling: "I want to play / I don't want to play again." On my first play of La Citta I did not enjoy it all. Was it the game or the "game experience" that left me so miserable? The argument that its not the game itself includes: Learning was difficult and took over an hour  before a single move was made. German cards without paste-ups made the first two years slow as each player asked again and again for clarification.

We were at the game for four hours total. With only 30 decision points per player (5 plays by 6 years) and few of them actually not-obvious (e.g. it is not a hard choice to build farms early before there is less space for it) it was about 10 minutes of downtime per meaningful choice at best. And unlike other long games, it felt as if most decisions were reactive. It is odd to say this about a game that has a weigth on bgg of 3 , but to me, it actually felt much lighter than other games this long. And if I am going to sit down with one game for 3  (or 4 !) hours I want the heavy-game feeling. 

On this isuue of "long-ness": not every long game feels long. But (again, perhaps because of the tough start we had) La Citta felt long, tedious, robotic. Each choice I made seemed to be a simple crunching of numbers: "how many black vs. white vs. blue? If this, then this. again, again." By the 4th year I was looking wistfully at the games being played around me. By the 5th and 6th year I had lost almost all interest in doing the number crunching of counting the arches next to my cities.

As I think about the exquisite anguish in a game like Clippers or Caylus-- the agony you feel as an opponant selects something you really wanted and you cry out, "Noooooo! Do anything but that!" I think that this quality-- the quality of caring so much you viscerally emote-- is something that is wholly missing in La Citta. It mattered little what my opponant did. They did it, and the next player reacted and so on. And to add a little insult to the whole injury, for a game this long there sure was a lot of randomness! The wide variation in effect of cards that can be drawn (for example the 3 white arches for 3 gold and its ilk) and the way in which the open cards start to stagnate and all the bad cards no one wants take up all the space, means that as soon as a single good card is flipped, the spoils flow to whoever is next in turn order.

I am willing to try it again. But in a way it reminded me of Tyros. A few nice things that just added up to a whole lot of not enough fun for a very long time.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Not as Euro as you would expect from a game called "The City" in Italian.  This one has some nice player conflict that separates it from the rest.  Components are beautiful, particularly the little plastic guys who come in two genders, a nice touch that is almost impossible to see unless you look at them very closely.  Gameplay is interesting, almost reminiscent of the military race in [gameid=25613], where you are trying to build up your economic engine, but you have to make sure that you defend yourself well enough, and also take the time to attack opponents effectively when they don't do the same.  The biggest drawback is that this game needs some kind of house rule to flush out the unused action cards.  I like using [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/filepage/2568/la-cittatxt]Joe Casadonte's variant[/url].

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Strategy game, with some luck and a lot of planning. Scales well. Nice bits.
Its flaws are:
I) the cards aren't balanced and so sometimes you buy a card and the card that is revealed to replace it (and thus is avaible for your opponent) is much better than all the 7 you had available.
II) the "Voice of the People" is utterly random. Once in a while you can mitigate it with a "Polling the People", even if there's nothing worse than a Poll (not paying the extra 2$) revealing 3 different cards... and I'm inclined to believe that 2$ and an action is a bit overpriced for the full effect, especially since the other players can simply "copy" your next moves. Bluffing won't help unless you're the last player and can thus do some "surprise" action in the last turn; but if you go last will all the other players ignore that "Polling the People"?
III) the "3 food" tile is overpowered. If it comes near a "2 food" tile, whoever goes first during the setup has got a great advantage with no drawback. This is very bad for a "strategy game".
IV) once a city is "doomed" (at least two neighbouring cities "stronger" than it), there's no hope to save it. Losing persons means losing builings, that means losing more persons during later turns and so on. This can easily happen in the first half of the game (and having to play three other turns with no hope to win can be acceptable for a 30-minutes card game, but not in a 2-hour strategy game!).

RATING: 7.5
"Unexpected" was what came to mind after my play of La Citta.  I was in fairly good shape going into and throughout the last round; I had plenty of food to feed my people as well as any small influx that may happen during the migration.  If I didn't end up first I was confident to at least come in a close second.  Then the unthinkable happened and culture (the worst possible choice) turned out to be the voice of the people.  So instead of the other players having to starve their population and take the negative hit in scores, I took their extra for them and I ended up dead last.

Sure one can make the argument that the random card draws cause the game to be somewhat chaotic, but for me I think that is part of the charm and the unpredictability of La Citta.  The placing of new cities and buildings can be very cutthroat, especially as prime real estates start to disappear and as one begin to encroach onto other's territories.  My only problem is how often you can get stuck with a bad set of political action cards and end up having to pick something suboptimal only to give the next person a good one in return.  I fear that decisions may become methodical with repeated plays, but for now it is a very tense and enjoyable 3-player game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
I was actually quite surprised with this game. I took a punt on this for a bargain price from The Works, took absolutely months (over a year in fact) to get it to the table, and when I finally did, found it was actually a pretty solid game.

Extremely easy once you get the hang of it, great interaction as you try to entice the population of a neighbours city into yours, and it's really awesome to see the population of everyones city expand.

Would I pay full price for this? Almost certainly not. But for £6.99 it's one of my greatest game to cost purchases ever.
